<th>Official website</th>

<p>The <b>Boston Bruins</b> are a professional <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> team based in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>. They are members of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Division_(NHL)" title="Atlantic Division (NHL)">Atlantic Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Conference_(NHL)" title="Eastern Conference (NHL)">Eastern Conference</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> (NHL). The team has been in existence since <a href="/wiki/1924%E2%80%9325_NHL_season" title="1924–25 NHL season">1924</a>, and is the league's third-oldest team and is the oldest in the United States. It is also an <a href="/wiki/Original_Six" title="Original Six">Original Six</a> franchise, along with the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Blackhawks" title="Chicago Blackhawks">Chicago Blackhawks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Red_Wings" title="Detroit Red Wings">Detroit Red Wings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leafs</a>. The Bruins have won six <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> championships, tied for fourth most of all-time with the Blackhawks and tied second-most of any American NHL team also with the Blackhawks (behind the Red Wings, who have 11). Their home arena is the <a href="/wiki/TD_Garden" title="TD Garden">TD Garden</a>, where they have played since <a href="/wiki/1995%E2%80%9396_NHL_season" title="1995–96 NHL season">1995</a>. The Bruins began play in the NHL on December 1, 1924. The first facility to host them was what was then known as the Boston Arena (today's <a href="/wiki/Matthews_Arena" title="Matthews Arena">Matthews Arena</a>) — the world's oldest (built 1909–10) indoor ice hockey facility still in use for the sport at <a href="/wiki/Hockey_East" title="Hockey East">any level of competition</a><sup id="cite_ref-gonu_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gonu-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fitz_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fitz-3">[3]</a></sup> — and following the Bruins' departure from the Boston Arena, the team played its home games at the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Garden" title="Boston Garden">Boston Garden</a> for 67 seasons, beginning in <a href="/wiki/1928%E2%80%9329_NHL_season" title="1928–29 NHL season">1928</a> and concluding in 1995.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-More_footnotes" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg/40px-Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg/60px-Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg/80px-Text_document_with_red_question_mark.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div>
</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-World_War_II_years">Pre-World War II years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-World War II years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eddie_shore_1939.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Eddie_shore_1939.JPG/170px-Eddie_shore_1939.JPG" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Eddie_shore_1939.JPG/255px-Eddie_shore_1939.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Eddie_shore_1939.JPG/340px-Eddie_shore_1939.JPG 2x" data-file-width="815" data-file-height="1122" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eddie_shore_1939.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Defenseman" class="mw-redirect" title="Defenseman">Defenseman</a> <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Shore" title="Eddie Shore">Eddie Shore</a> was the team's first great star, making his debut in 1926.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1924,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> at the convincing of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> grocery tycoon <a href="/wiki/Charles_Adams_(ice_hockey)" title="Charles Adams (ice hockey)">Charles Adams</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> decided to expand to the United States. Adams had fallen in love with hockey while watching the <a href="/wiki/1924_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1924 Stanley Cup Finals">1924 Stanley Cup Finals</a> between the NHL champion <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada_Hockey_League" title="Western Canada Hockey League">WCHL</a> champion <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Tigers" title="Calgary Tigers">Calgary Tigers</a>. He persuaded the NHL to grant him a franchise for Boston, which occurred on November 1, 1924. With the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Maroons" title="Montreal Maroons">Montreal Maroons</a>, the team was one of the NHL's first expansion teams, and the first NHL team to be based in the United States.</p>
<p>Adams' first act was to hire <a href="/wiki/Art_Ross" title="Art Ross">Art Ross</a>, a former star player and innovator, as general manager. Ross was the face of the franchise for the next thirty years, including four separate stints as coach.</p>
<p>Adams directed Ross to come up with a nickname that would portray an untamed animal displaying speed, agility, and cunning. Ross came up with "Bruins", an Old English word used for <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bears</a> (from the Dutch "bruin" meaning brown) in classic folk-tales. The team's bearlike nickname also went along with the team's original uniform colors of brown and yellow, which came from Adams' grocery chain, <a href="/wiki/Finast" title="Finast">First National Stores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 1, 1924, the new Bruins team played their first NHL game against the Maroons, at <a href="/wiki/Boston_Arena" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Arena">Boston Arena</a>, with the Bruins winning the game by a 2–1 score. But the team only managed a 6–24–0 record (for last place) in its first season. They played three more seasons at the Arena, after which the Bruins became the main tenant of the famous <a href="/wiki/Boston_Garden" title="Boston Garden">Boston Garden</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> while the old Boston Arena facility — the world's oldest existing indoor ice hockey venue <a href="/wiki/Hockey_East#Conference_arenas" title="Hockey East">still used for the sport</a> at any level of competition, and the only surviving rink where an Original Six NHL team began their career in the league — was eventually taken over by <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_University" title="Northeastern University">Northeastern University</a>, and renamed <a href="/wiki/Matthews_Arena" title="Matthews Arena">Matthews Arena</a> when the university renovated it in 1979.</p>
<p>In their third season, <a href="/wiki/1926%E2%80%9327_NHL_season" title="1926–27 NHL season">1926–27</a>, the team markedly improved. Ross took advantage of the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hockey_League" title="Western Hockey League">Western Hockey League</a> to purchase several western stars, including the team's first great star, a <a href="/wiki/Defenseman" class="mw-redirect" title="Defenseman">defenseman</a> from <a href="/wiki/Fort_Qu%27Appelle,_Saskatchewan" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Qu'Appelle, Saskatchewan">Fort Qu'Appelle, Saskatchewan</a> named <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Shore" title="Eddie Shore">Eddie Shore</a>. The Bruins reached the <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cup</a> Final despite finishing only one game above .500, but lost to the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Senators_(original)" title="Ottawa Senators (original)">Ottawa Senators</a> in the first Cup Final to be between exclusively NHL teams. In 1929 the Bruins defeated the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> to win their first Stanley Cup. Standout players on the first championship team included Shore, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Oliver_(ice_hockey)" title="Harry Oliver (ice hockey)">Harry Oliver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dit_Clapper" title="Dit Clapper">Dit Clapper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Gainor" title="Dutch Gainor">Dutch Gainor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goaltender" title="Goaltender">goaltender</a> <a href="/wiki/Tiny_Thompson" title="Tiny Thompson">Tiny Thompson</a>. The 1928–29 season was the first played at <a href="/wiki/Boston_Garden" title="Boston Garden">Boston Garden</a>, which Adams had built after guaranteeing his backers $500,000 in gate receipts over the next five years.</p>
<p>The season after that, <a href="/wiki/1929%E2%80%9330_NHL_season" title="1929–30 NHL season">1929–30</a>, the Bruins posted the best-ever regular season winning percentage in the NHL (an astonishing .875, winning 38 out of 44 games, a record which still stands) and shattered numerous team scoring records, but lost to the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a> in the Cup Final.</p>
<p>The 1930s Bruins teams included Shore, Thompson, Clapper, <a href="/wiki/Babe_Siebert" title="Babe Siebert">Babe Siebert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cooney_Weiland" title="Cooney Weiland">Cooney Weiland</a>. The team led the league's standings five times in the decade. In <a href="/wiki/1938%E2%80%9339_NHL_season" title="1938–39 NHL season">1939</a>, the team changed its uniform colors from brown and yellow to the current black and gold, and captured the second Stanley Cup in franchise history. That year, Thompson was traded for <a href="/wiki/Rookie" title="Rookie">rookie</a> goaltender <a href="/wiki/Frank_Brimsek" title="Frank Brimsek">Frank Brimsek</a>. Brimsek had an award-winning season, capturing the <a href="/wiki/Vezina_Trophy" title="Vezina Trophy">Vezina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calder_Memorial_Trophy" title="Calder Memorial Trophy">Calder Trophies</a>, becoming the first rookie named to the NHL First All-Star Team, and earning the nickname "Mr. Zero." The team skating in front of Brimsek included <a href="/wiki/Bill_Cowley" title="Bill Cowley">Bill Cowley</a>, Shore, Clapper and "Sudden Death" <a href="/wiki/Mel_Hill" title="Mel Hill">Mel Hill</a> (who scored three <a href="/wiki/Overtime_(ice_hockey)" title="Overtime (ice hockey)">overtime</a> goals in one playoff series), together with the "<a href="/wiki/Kraut_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Kraut Line">Kraut Line</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Centre_(ice_hockey)" title="Centre (ice hockey)">center</a> <a href="/wiki/Milt_Schmidt" title="Milt Schmidt">Milt Schmidt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winger_(ice_hockey)" title="Winger (ice hockey)">right winger</a> <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Bauer" title="Bobby Bauer">Bobby Bauer</a> and left winger <a href="/wiki/Woody_Dumart" title="Woody Dumart">Woody Dumart</a>.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1939%E2%80%9340_NHL_season" title="1939–40 NHL season">1940</a> Shore was traded to the struggling <a href="/wiki/New_York_Americans" title="New York Americans">New York Americans</a> for his final NHL season. In <a href="/wiki/1940%E2%80%9341_NHL_season" title="1940–41 NHL season">1941</a> the Bruins won their third Stanley Cup after losing only eight games and finishing first in the regular season. It was their last Stanley Cup for 29 years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_the_.22Original_Six.22_era">World War II and the "Original Six" era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World War II and the &quot;Original Six&quot; era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>World War II affected the Bruins more than most teams; Brimsek and the "Krauts" all enlisted after the <a href="/wiki/1940%E2%80%9341_NHL_season" title="1940–41 NHL season">1940–41&#160;Cup win</a>, and lost the most productive years of their careers at war. Cowley, assisted by veteran player Clapper and <a href="/wiki/Busher_Jackson" title="Busher Jackson">Busher Jackson</a>, was the team's remaining star. Even though the NHL had by <a href="/wiki/1943%E2%80%9344_NHL_season" title="1943–44 NHL season">1943</a> been reduced to the six teams that would in the modern era be called the "<a href="/wiki/Original_Six" title="Original Six">Original Six</a>", talent was depleted enough that freak seasons could take place, as in <a href="/wiki/1943%E2%80%9344_NHL_season" title="1943–44 NHL season">1944</a>, when Bruin <a href="/wiki/Herb_Cain" title="Herb Cain">Herb Cain</a> would set the then-NHL record for points in a season with 82. But the Bruins did not make the playoffs that season, and Cain was out of the NHL two seasons later.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Miltschmidt.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Miltschmidt.png/125px-Miltschmidt.png" width="125" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Miltschmidt.png/188px-Miltschmidt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Miltschmidt.png/250px-Miltschmidt.png 2x" data-file-width="788" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Miltschmidt.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Milt_Schmidt" title="Milt Schmidt">Milt Schmidt</a>, oldest living <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hockey Hall of Fame">Hockey Hall of Famer</a> and the captain of the Bruins in the early 1950s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The stars returned for the <a href="/wiki/1945%E2%80%9346_NHL_season" title="1945–46 NHL season">1945–46 season</a>, and Clapper led the team back to the Stanley Cup Final as player-coach. He retired as a player after the next season, becoming the first player to play twenty NHL seasons, but stayed on as coach for two more years. Brimsek proved to be not as good as he was before the war, and after 1946 the Bruins lost in the first playoff round three straight years, resulting in Clapper's resignation. Brimsek was traded to the last-place <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Blackhawks" title="Chicago Blackhawks">Chicago Black Hawks</a> in <a href="/wiki/1948%E2%80%9349_NHL_season" title="1948–49 NHL season">1949</a>, followed by the unexpected lifetime ban of promising young star <a href="/wiki/Don_Gallinger" title="Don Gallinger">Don Gallinger</a> on suspicion of gambling. The only remaining quality young player who stayed with the team for any length was forward <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Peirson" title="Johnny Peirson">Johnny Peirson</a>, recognizable to fans of a later era as the Bruins' television color commentator in the 1970s.</p>
<p>During the 1948–49 season, the original form of the "spoked-B" logo, with a small number "24" to the left of the capital B signifying the calendar year in the 20th century in which the Bruins team first played, and a similarly small "49" to the right of the "B", for the then-current season's calendar year in the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> appeared on their home uniforms—a nod to the Boston area's nickname of "The Hub". The following season, the logo was modified into the basic "spoked-B" form that was to be used, virtually unchanged (except for certain proportions within the logo), through the 1993–94 season.</p>
<p>The 1950s began with Charles Adams' son <a href="/wiki/Weston_Adams" title="Weston Adams">Weston</a> (who had been team president since 1936) facing financial trouble. He was forced to accept a buyout offer from <a href="/wiki/Walter_A._Brown" title="Walter A. Brown">Walter A. Brown</a>, the owner of the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Boston_Celtics" title="Boston Celtics">Boston Celtics</a> and the Garden, in 1951. Although there were some instances of success (such as making the Stanley Cup Final in <a href="/wiki/1952%E2%80%9353_NHL_season" title="1952–53 NHL season">1953</a>, <a href="/wiki/1956%E2%80%9357_NHL_season" title="1956–57 NHL season">1957</a> and <a href="/wiki/1957%E2%80%9358_NHL_season" title="1957–58 NHL season">1958</a>, only to lose to the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a> each time), the Bruins mustered only four winning seasons between 1947 and 1967. They missed the playoffs eight straight years between 1960 and 1967.</p>
<p>In 1954, on New Year's Day, Robert Skrak, an assistant to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Zamboni" title="Frank Zamboni">Frank Zamboni</a>, the inventor of the best known <a href="/wiki/Ice_resurfacer" title="Ice resurfacer">ice resurfacing</a> machine of the time, demonstrated a very early model of the machine at Boston Garden to the team management, and as a result, the Bruins ordered one of the then-produced "Model E" resurfacers to be used at the Garden, the first known NHL team to acquire one of the soon-to-be-ubiquitous "Zambonis" for their own use. The Bruins' Zamboni Model E, factory serial number 21—used as late as the 1980s on an emergency basis—eventually ended up in the <a href="/wiki/Hockey_Hall_of_Fame" title="Hockey Hall of Fame">Hockey Hall of Fame</a> in Toronto in 1988 for preservation.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 18, 1958, a milestone in NHL history occurred as the first black person ever to play in the NHL stepped onto the ice for the Bruins, <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>-born left wing <a href="/wiki/Willie_O%27Ree" title="Willie O'Ree">Willie O'Ree</a>. He played in 45 games for the Bruins over the <a href="/wiki/1957%E2%80%9358_NHL_season" title="1957–58 NHL season">1957–58</a> and <a href="/wiki/1960%E2%80%9361_NHL_season" title="1960–61 NHL season">1960–61</a> seasons, scoring six goals and ten assists in his NHL career.</p>
<p>During this period, the farm system of the Bruins was not as expansive or well-developed as most of the other five teams. The Bruins sought players not protected by the other teams, and in like fashion to the aforementioned signing of Willie O'Ree, the team signed <a href="/wiki/Tom_Williams_(ice_hockey,_born_1940)" title="Tom Williams (ice hockey, born 1940)">Tommy Williams</a> from the 1960 Olympic-gold medal winning American national men's hockey team—at the time the only American player in the NHL—in <a href="/wiki/1962%E2%80%9363_NHL_season" title="1962–63 NHL season">1962</a>. The "Uke Line"—named for the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukrainian</a> heritage of <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Bucyk" title="Johnny Bucyk">Johnny Bucyk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vic_Stasiuk" title="Vic Stasiuk">Vic Stasiuk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronco_Horvath" title="Bronco Horvath">Bronco Horvath</a> — came to Boston in 1957 and enjoyed four productive offensive seasons, heralding, along with scoring stalwarts <a href="/wiki/Don_McKenney" title="Don McKenney">Don McKenney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fleming_MacKell" class="mw-redirect" title="Fleming MacKell">Fleming MacKell</a>, the successful era of the late 1950s. There followed a long and difficult reconstruction period in the early, mid-1960s.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_and_the_Big_Bad_Bruins">Expansion and the Big Bad Bruins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Expansion and the Big Bad Bruins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Weston Adams repurchased the Bruins in 1964 after Brown's death and set about rebuilding the team. Adams signed a defenseman from <a href="/wiki/Parry_Sound,_Ontario" title="Parry Sound, Ontario">Parry Sound, Ontario</a>, named <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Orr" title="Bobby Orr">Bobby Orr</a>, who entered the league in <a href="/wiki/1966%E2%80%9367_NHL_season" title="1966–67 NHL season">1966</a> and became, in the eyes of many, the greatest player of all time. He was announced that season's winner of the <a href="/wiki/Calder_Memorial_Trophy" title="Calder Memorial Trophy">Calder Memorial Trophy</a> for Rookie of the Year and named to the Second NHL All-Star Team. When asked about Orr's NHL debut game, October 19, 1966, against the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Red_Wings" title="Detroit Red Wings">Detroit Red Wings</a>, then-Bruins coach <a href="/wiki/Harry_Sinden" title="Harry Sinden">Harry Sinden</a> recalled:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"Our fans had heard about this kid for a few years now. There was a lot of pressure on him, but he met all the expectations. He was a star from the moment they played the <a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">national anthem</a> in the opening game of the season."</dd>
</dl>
<p>The Bruins then obtained young forwards <a href="/wiki/Phil_Esposito" title="Phil Esposito">Phil Esposito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Hodge" title="Ken Hodge">Ken Hodge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Stanfield" title="Fred Stanfield">Fred Stanfield</a> from <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> in a deal celebrated as one of the most one-sided in hockey history. Hodge and Stanfield became key elements of the Bruins' success, and Esposito, who centered a line with Hodge and <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Cashman" title="Wayne Cashman">Wayne Cashman</a>, became the league's top goal-scorer and the first NHL player to break the 100-point mark, setting many goal- and point-scoring records. Esposito remains one of four players to win the <a href="/wiki/Art_Ross_Trophy" title="Art Ross Trophy">Art Ross Trophy</a> four consecutive seasons (the other three are <a href="/wiki/Jarom%C3%ADr_J%C3%A1gr" title="Jaromír Jágr">Jaromir Jagr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Gretzky" title="Wayne Gretzky">Wayne Gretzky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gordie_Howe" title="Gordie Howe">Gordie Howe</a>). With other stars like forwards Bucyk, <a href="/wiki/John_McKenzie_(hockey_player)" class="mw-redirect" title="John McKenzie (hockey player)">John McKenzie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derek_Sanderson" title="Derek Sanderson">Derek Sanderson</a>, and Hodge, steady defenders like <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Smith" title="Dallas Smith">Dallas Smith</a> and goaltender <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Cheevers" title="Gerry Cheevers">Gerry Cheevers</a>, the "Big Bad Bruins" became one of the league's top teams from the late 1960s into the 1980s.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_(1970).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_%281970%29.jpg/225px-Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_%281970%29.jpg" width="225" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_%281970%29.jpg/338px-Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_%281970%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_%281970%29.jpg/450px-Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_%281970%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="602" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bobby_Orr_in_mid-air_(1970).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bobby_Orr" title="Bobby Orr">Orr</a> is tripped and flies through the air after scoring "The Goal" in overtime to win the <a href="/wiki/1970_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1970 Stanley Cup Finals">1970 Stanley Cup Finals</a>. The image is widely considered to be one of the most famous in hockey history.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1969%E2%80%9370_NHL_season" title="1969–70 NHL season">1970</a>, a 29-year Stanley Cup drought came to an end in Boston, as the Bruins defeated the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Blues" title="St. Louis Blues">St. Louis Blues</a> in four games in the Final. Orr scored the game-winning goal in overtime to clinch the Stanley Cup. The same season was Orr's most awarded—the third of eight consecutive years he won the <a href="/wiki/James_Norris_Memorial_Trophy" title="James Norris Memorial Trophy">James Norris Memorial Trophy</a> as the top defenseman in the NHL—and he won the <a href="/wiki/Art_Ross_Trophy" title="Art Ross Trophy">Art Ross Trophy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Conn_Smythe_Trophy" title="Conn Smythe Trophy">Conn Smythe Trophy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hart_Memorial_Trophy" title="Hart Memorial Trophy">Hart Memorial Trophy</a>, the only player to ever win four major awards in the same season.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"No one, absolutely no one, could have finished a goal in like manner. For years Orr had been described as someone who was graceful, elegant, powerful, without fear—poetry in motion. All these epithets were captured and immortalized in the photos of the goal that won the 1970 Stanley Cup."<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1970%E2%80%9371_NHL_season" title="1970–71 NHL season">1970–71 season</a> was, in retrospect, the high-water mark of the 1970s for Boston. While Sinden temporarily retired from hockey to enter business (he was replaced by ex-Bruin and Canadien defenseman <a href="/wiki/Tom_Johnson_(ice_hockey)" title="Tom Johnson (ice hockey)">Tom Johnson</a>), the Bruins set dozens of offensive scoring records: they had seven of the league's top ten scorers—a feat not achieved before or since—set the record for wins in a season, and in a league that had never seen a 100-point scorer before <a href="/wiki/1968%E2%80%9369_NHL_season" title="1968–69 NHL season">1969</a>, the Bruins had four that year. All four (Orr, Esposito, Bucyk and Hodge) were named First Team All-Stars, a feat matched in the expansion era only by the <a href="/wiki/1976%E2%80%9377_NHL_season" title="1976–77 NHL season">1976–77</a> Canadiens. Boston were favored to repeat as Cup champions, but ran into a roadblock in the playoffs. Up 5–1 at one point in game two of the quarter-finals against the Canadiens (and rookie goaltender <a href="/wiki/Ken_Dryden" title="Ken Dryden">Ken Dryden</a>), the Bruins squandered the lead to lose 7–5. The Bruins never recovered and lost the series in seven games.</p>
<p>While the Bruins were not quite as dominant the next season (although only three points behind the 1971 pace), Esposito and Orr were once again one-two in the scoring standings (followed by Bucyk in ninth place) and they regained the Stanley Cup by defeating the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> in six games in the Finals. Rangers blueliner <a href="/wiki/Brad_Park" title="Brad Park">Brad Park</a>, who came runner-up to Orr's (then) five-year monopoly on the Norris Trophy, said, "Bobby Orr <i>was</i>—didn't make—the difference."</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1972%E2%80%9373_Boston_Bruins_season" title="1972–73 Boston Bruins season">1972–73 season</a> saw upheaval at the Bruins. Former head coach Sinden became the general manager. Bruins players <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Cheevers" title="Gerry Cheevers">Gerry Cheevers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derek_Sanderson" title="Derek Sanderson">Derek Sanderson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johnny_McKenzie" class="mw-redirect" title="Johnny McKenzie">Johnny McKenzie</a> left to join the upstart <a href="/wiki/World_Hockey_Association" title="World Hockey Association">World Hockey Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDryden200075_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDryden200075-11">[11]</a></sup> Coach <a href="/wiki/Tom_Johnson_(ice_hockey)" title="Tom Johnson (ice hockey)">Tom Johnson</a> was fired 52 games into the season, replaced by <a href="/wiki/Bep_Guidolin" title="Bep Guidolin">Bep Guidolin</a>, who had once coached Orr. The Adams family, which had owned the team since its founding in the 1920s, sold it to <a href="/wiki/Storer_Broadcasting" class="mw-redirect" title="Storer Broadcasting">Storer Broadcasting</a>. The Bruins' season came to a premature end in a first-round loss to the Rangers in the <a href="/wiki/1973_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" class="mw-redirect" title="1973 Stanley Cup playoffs">1973 playoffs</a>, losing Esposito to injury in that first round.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrunt200653.E2.80.93254_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrunt200653.E2.80.93254-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1974, the Bruins regained their first place standing in the regular season, with three 100-point scorers on the team (Esposito, Orr, and Hodge). However they lost the <a href="/wiki/1973%E2%80%9374_NHL_season" title="1973–74 NHL season">1974</a> Final in an upset to the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Flyers" title="Philadelphia Flyers">Philadelphia Flyers</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Don_Cherry" title="Don Cherry">Don Cherry</a> stepped behind the bench as the new coach in <a href="/wiki/1974%E2%80%9375_NHL_season" title="1974–75 NHL season">1974–75</a>. The Bruins stocked themselves with <a href="/wiki/Enforcer_(hockey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enforcer (hockey)">enforcers</a> and grinders, and remained competitive under Cherry's reign, the so-called "Lunch Pail A.C"., behind players such as <a href="/wiki/Gregg_Sheppard" title="Gregg Sheppard">Gregg Sheppard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terry_O%27Reilly" title="Terry O'Reilly">Terry O'Reilly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stan_Jonathan" title="Stan Jonathan">Stan Jonathan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_McNab" title="Peter McNab">Peter McNab</a>. This would also turn out to be Orr's final full season in the league, before his knee injuries worsened, as well as the last time Orr and Esposito would finish 1-2 in regular season scoring. The Bruins placed second in the Adams Division, and lost to the <a href="/wiki/1974%E2%80%9375_Chicago_Black_Hawks_season" title="1974–75 Chicago Black Hawks season">Chicago Black Hawks</a> in the first round of the <a href="/wiki/1975_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" class="mw-redirect" title="1975 Stanley Cup playoffs">1975 playoffs</a>, losing a best-of-three series, two games to one.</p>
<p>Continuing with Cherry's rebuilding of the team, the Bruins traded Esposito and <a href="/wiki/Carol_Vadnais" title="Carol Vadnais">Carol Vadnais</a> for <a href="/wiki/Brad_Park" title="Brad Park">Brad Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ratelle" title="Jean Ratelle">Jean Ratelle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Zanussi" title="Joe Zanussi">Joe Zanussi</a> to the Rangers. That trade was particularly controversial for both Bruins and Rangers fans, as Esposito was one of the most popular Bruins players (though it was known he disagreed with Cherry's coaching), while Park and Ratelle were Rangers stalwarts.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, Boston ended up getting the better of the trade, as Esposito never reached his previous scoring highs with the Rangers, while Ratelle maintained his skill level with the Bruins and was a high scorer for several years more. Particularly, it was Park who reemerged as one of the league's best defencemen and filled the void left by Orr, who had been sidelined by surgery at the start of 1975–76 and only managed to play ten games before being injured and lost for the rest of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The Bruins made the semi-finals again, losing to the Flyers.</p>
<p>As an impending free agent, contract talks with Orr and his agent <a href="/wiki/Alan_Eagleson" title="Alan Eagleson">Alan Eagleson</a> had been tumultuous throughout 1975–76. Although insurers would not underwrite Orr and doctors advised he could not play much longer, the Bruins still attempted to re-sign Orr and offered him a five-year deal at US$925,000, or 18.6 percent ownership of the club in 1980. However, Eagleson turned down the offer without informing Orr, instead signing him to the Chicago Black Hawks in <a href="/wiki/1975%E2%80%9376_NHL_season" title="1975–76 NHL season">1976</a>; Orr was never effective — having only played 26 games in Chicago — and retired after many knee operations in <a href="/wiki/1978%E2%80%9379_NHL_season" title="1978–79 NHL season">1979</a>.</p>
<p>Cheevers returned from the WHA in <a href="/wiki/1976%E2%80%9377_NHL_season" title="1976–77 NHL season">1977</a>, and the Bruins got past the Flyers in the semifinals, but they were swept by the Canadiens in the Stanley Cup Finals. The story repeated itself in <a href="/wiki/1977%E2%80%9378_NHL_season" title="1977–78 NHL season">1978</a>—with a balanced attack that saw Boston have eleven players with 20+ goal seasons, still the NHL record—as the Bruins made the Final once more, but lost in six games to the Canadiens team that had recorded the best regular season in modern history. After that series, Johnny Bucyk retired, holding virtually every Bruins' career longevity and scoring mark to that time.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1978%E2%80%9379_NHL_season" title="1978–79 NHL season">1979</a> semi-final series against the Habs proved to be Cherry's undoing. In the deciding seventh game, the Bruins, up by a goal, were called for having too many men on the ice in the late stages of the third period. Montreal tied the game on the ensuing power play and won in overtime. Never popular with Harry Sinden, by then the Bruins' general manager, Cherry was dismissed as head coach but was later hired in the same capacity with the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies_(NHL)" title="Colorado Rockies (NHL)">Colorado Rockies</a>.</p>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> on December 23, 1979, just after the Bruins beat the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> 4-3, John Kaptain, a Rangers fan from New Jersey, stole <a href="/wiki/Stan_Jonathan" title="Stan Jonathan">Stan Jonathan</a>'s stick and hit him with it during a post-game scrum. When other fans got involved, <a href="/wiki/Terry_O%27Reilly" title="Terry O'Reilly">Terry O'Reilly</a> charged into the stands followed by his teammates. During the fight, Bruin defenseman and future NBC hockey analyst <a href="/wiki/Mike_Milbury" title="Mike Milbury">Mike Milbury</a> famously pulled off Kaptain's shoe and hit him with it. O'Reilly, a future team captain, received an eight-game suspension for the brawl, while Milbury and McNab were suspended for six games and the entire team except for Cheevers was fined $500, while Kaptain was sentenced to six months in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In 2012, <a href="/wiki/The_Sports_Network" title="The Sports Network">TSN</a> named the fight, entitled "Milbury and The Shoe", as #4 on its Top 10 Craziest Hockey Moments.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s_and_1990s">1980s and 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1980s and 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:417px;max-width:417px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:137px;max-width:137px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Logo_(Home_1949-1995).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/23/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Home_1949-1995%29.svg/135px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Home_1949-1995%29.svg.png" width="135" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/23/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Home_1949-1995%29.svg/203px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Home_1949-1995%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/23/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Home_1949-1995%29.svg/270px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Home_1949-1995%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="216" data-file-height="216" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Boston Bruins logo (1949–1995). Primary logo, used on white jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:137px;max-width:137px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Logo_(Away_1955-1995).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c7/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Away_1955-1995%29.svg/135px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Away_1955-1995%29.svg.png" width="135" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c7/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Away_1955-1995%29.svg/203px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Away_1955-1995%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c7/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Away_1955-1995%29.svg/270px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Away_1955-1995%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="216" data-file-height="216" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Boston Bruins logo (1955–1957, 1959–1965, 1967–1995, alternate 2006–07 season). Secondary logo, used on black jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:137px;max-width:137px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Logo_(Alternate_1955-1995).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Alternate_1955-1995%29.svg/135px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Alternate_1955-1995%29.svg.png" width="135" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Alternate_1955-1995%29.svg/203px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Alternate_1955-1995%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Alternate_1955-1995%29.svg/270px-Boston_Bruins_Logo_%28Alternate_1955-1995%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="216" data-file-height="216" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Boston Bruins logo (1955–1957, 1959–1967). Secondary logo, used on gold jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1979%E2%80%9380_NHL_season" title="1979–80 NHL season">1979 season</a> saw new Head Coach <a href="/wiki/Fred_Creighton" title="Fred Creighton">Fred Creighton</a>—himself replaced by the newly retired Cheevers the following year. The Bruins trade of <a href="/wiki/Ron_Grahame" title="Ron Grahame">Ron Grahame</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Kings" title="Los Angeles Kings">Los Angeles Kings</a> for a first round pick, that turned out to be eighth overall, enabled the Bruins to draft <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bourque" title="Ray Bourque">Ray Bourque</a>, one of the greatest defensemen of all-time and the face of the Bruins for over two decades.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Bruins made the playoffs every year through the 1980s behind stars such as Park, Bourque and <a href="/wiki/Rick_Middleton" title="Rick Middleton">Rick Middleton</a>—and had the league's best record in <a href="/wiki/1982%E2%80%9383_NHL_season" title="1982–83 NHL season">1983</a> behind a <a href="/wiki/Vezina_Trophy" title="Vezina Trophy">Vezina Trophy</a>–winning season from ex-Flyer goaltender <a href="/wiki/Pete_Peeters" title="Pete Peeters">Pete Peeters</a>—but always fell short of making the Finals.</p>
<p>Bourque, <a href="/wiki/Cam_Neely" title="Cam Neely">Cam Neely</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Crowder" title="Keith Crowder">Keith Crowder</a> led the Bruins to another Cup Final appearance in <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NHL_season" title="1987–88 NHL season">1988</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Oilers" title="Edmonton Oilers">Edmonton Oilers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The Bruins lost in a four-game sweep, but a memorable moment in the would-be fourth game ensued, when in the second period with the game tied 3–3, a blown fuse put the lights out at the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Garden" title="Boston Garden">Boston Garden</a>. The rest of the game was cancelled and the series shifted to Edmonton. The Oilers completed the sweep, 6–3, back at <a href="/wiki/Northlands_Coliseum" title="Northlands Coliseum">Northlands Coliseum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a> in what was originally scheduled as Game Five. The event is considered to be the reason the Bruins began work on a new arena.</p>
<p>Boston returned to the Stanley Cup Final in <a href="/wiki/1989%E2%80%9390_NHL_season" title="1989–90 NHL season">1990</a> (with Neely, Bourque, <a href="/wiki/Craig_Janney" title="Craig Janney">Craig Janney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Carpenter_(ice_hockey)" title="Bobby Carpenter (ice hockey)">Bobby Carpenter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rookie" title="Rookie">rookie</a> <a href="/wiki/Don_Sweeney" title="Don Sweeney">Don Sweeney</a>, and former Oiler goalie <a href="/wiki/Andy_Moog" title="Andy Moog">Andy Moog</a> and <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9jean_Lemelin" title="Réjean Lemelin">Reggie Lemelin</a> splitting goaltending duties), but again lost to the Oilers, this time in five games.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bourque_7.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bourque_7.jpg/225px-Bourque_7.jpg" width="225" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Bourque_7.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bourque_7.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ray_Bourque" title="Ray Bourque">Ray Bourque</a>, shown in 1981 and before switching to his familiar No. 77, led the Bruins to two Stanley Cup Finals appearances in <a href="/wiki/1988_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1988 Stanley Cup Finals">1988</a> and <a href="/wiki/1990_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1990 Stanley Cup Finals">1990</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NHL_season" title="1987–88 NHL season">1987–88 NHL season</a> — when the Bruins finally broke <a href="/wiki/Bruins-Canadiens_rivalry#All-Time_Postseason_Series_Results" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruins-Canadiens rivalry">the Canadiens' 57-year-long (1930–87) playoff win streak</a> against them — through the <a href="/wiki/1989%E2%80%9390_NHL_season" title="1989–90 NHL season">1990</a>, <a href="/wiki/1990%E2%80%9391_NHL_season" title="1990–91 NHL season">1991</a>, <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_NHL_season" title="1991–92 NHL season">1992</a> and <a href="/wiki/1993%E2%80%9394_NHL_season" title="1993–94 NHL season">1994</a> seasons, the Bruins began to amass a playoff series winning streak of their own, in defeating their <a href="/wiki/Original_Six" title="Original Six">Original Six</a> nemesis Montreal Canadiens in the playoffs, getting some revenge for a rivalry which had been lopsided in the Canadiens' favour in playoff action, with Montreal having won 18 consecutive playoff series against the Bruins between 1946 and 1987. In <a href="/wiki/1990%E2%80%9391_NHL_season" title="1990–91 NHL season">1991</a> and <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_NHL_season" title="1991–92 NHL season">1992</a>, the Bruins suffered two consecutive Conference Final losses to the eventual Cup champion, the <a href="/wiki/Mario_Lemieux" title="Mario Lemieux">Mario Lemieux</a>–led <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a>.</p>
<p>Starting from the <a href="/wiki/1992%E2%80%9393_NHL_season" title="1992–93 NHL season">1992–93 NHL season</a> onwards, the Bruins had not gotten past the second round of the playoffs until winning the Stanley Cup after the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_NHL_season" title="2010–11 NHL season">2011</a> season.</p>
<p>The 1992–93 season ended disappointingly for several reasons. Despite finishing with the second-best regular season record after Pittsburgh, Boston was swept in the first-round by the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Sabres" title="Buffalo Sabres">Buffalo Sabres</a>. During the post-season awards ceremony, Bruin players finished as runner-up on many of the honors—Bourque for the Norris, Oates for the Art Ross and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Byng_Trophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Byng Trophy">Lady Byng Trophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A9_Juneau" title="Joé Juneau">Joe Juneau</a> (who had broken the NHL record for assists in a season by a left-winger, a mark he still holds) for the Calder Trophy, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Poulin" title="Dave Poulin">Dave Poulin</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Selke_Trophy" title="Frank J. Selke Trophy">Frank J. Selke Trophy</a>, Moog for the <a href="/wiki/William_M._Jennings_Trophy" title="William M. Jennings Trophy">William M. Jennings Trophy</a> and coach <a href="/wiki/Brian_Sutter" title="Brian Sutter">Brian Sutter</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Jack_Adams_Award" title="Jack Adams Award">Jack Adams Award</a>. Poulin did win the <a href="/wiki/King_Clancy_Memorial_Trophy" title="King Clancy Memorial Trophy">King Clancy Memorial Trophy</a>, while Bourque made the NHL All-Star First Team and Juneau the NHL All-Rookie Team.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_NHL_season" title="1994–95 NHL season">1995 season</a> would be the Bruins' last at the Boston Garden. The final official match played in the Garden was a 3–0 loss to the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Devils" title="New Jersey Devils">New Jersey Devils</a> in the 1995 playoffs; the Bruins went on to play the final game at the fabled arena on September 28, 1995, in an exhibition matchup against the Canadiens. They subsequently moved into the FleetCenter, now known as the <a href="/wiki/TD_Garden" title="TD Garden">TD Garden</a>. In the 1996 playoffs, the Bruins lost their first-round series to the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panthers" title="Florida Panthers">Florida Panthers</a> in five games.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1996%E2%80%9397_NHL_season" title="1996–97 NHL season">1997</a>, Boston missed the playoffs for the first time in 30 years (and for the first time in the expansion era), having set the North American major professional record for most consecutive seasons in the playoffs. The Bruins lost in the first round of the 1998 playoffs to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Capitals" title="Washington Capitals">Washington Capitals</a> in six games. In 1999, the Bruins defeated the <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Hurricanes" title="Carolina Hurricanes">Carolina Hurricanes</a> in six games during the first round of the playoffs; nevertheless, they would lose to the Sabres in six games in the second round of the playoffs.</p>
<p>Historically, their most bitter rivals have been the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a>, whom the Bruins have played a record 34 times in the playoffs. The Bruins were formerly rivals to the now <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Hurricanes" title="Carolina Hurricanes">relocated</a> <a href="/wiki/Hartford_Whalers" title="Hartford Whalers">Hartford Whalers</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1999%E2%80%932000_NHL_season" title="1999–2000 NHL season">1999–2000</a> season, the Bruins finished in last place in the Northeast Division and failed to qualify for the playoffs. During a game between the Bruins and the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks" title="Vancouver Canucks">Vancouver Canucks</a> on February 21, 2000, <a href="/wiki/Marty_McSorley" title="Marty McSorley">Marty McSorley</a> was ejected for using his stick to hit Canucks forward <a href="/wiki/Donald_Brashear" title="Donald Brashear">Donald Brashear</a> in the head. McSorely was initially suspended for the remaining 23 games of the season. However, on October 6, McSorely was convicted of assault using a weapon for his attack on Brashear; he was then sentenced to 18 months of probation. As a result of the conviction, McSorley's suspension was extended through February 21, 2001.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a6/Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg/150px-Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg.png" width="150" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a6/Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg/225px-Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a6/Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg/300px-Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Old_Logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The primary logo used from 1995–2007. It contains design elements present in both the 1949–1995 and 2007–present logos.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After a 3–4–1 start, the Bruins fired head coach <a href="/wiki/Pat_Burns" title="Pat Burns">Pat Burns</a> and went with <a href="/wiki/Mike_Keenan" title="Mike Keenan">Mike Keenan</a> for the rest of the way. Despite a 15-point improvement from the previous season, the Bruins missed the playoffs in <a href="/wiki/2000%E2%80%9301_NHL_season" title="2000–01 NHL season">2000–01</a> by just one point, and Keenan was let go. Centerman <a href="/wiki/Jason_Allison" title="Jason Allison">Jason Allison</a> led the Bruins in scoring.</p>
<p>The following season, <a href="/wiki/2001%E2%80%9302_NHL_season" title="2001–02 NHL season">2001–02</a>, the Bruins improved again with another 13 points, winning their first Northeast Division title since <a href="/wiki/1992%E2%80%9393_NHL_season" title="1992–93 NHL season">1993</a> with a core built around <a href="/wiki/Joe_Thornton" title="Joe Thornton">Joe Thornton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Samsonov" title="Sergei Samsonov">Sergei Samsonov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Rolston" title="Brian Rolston">Brian Rolston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Guerin" title="Bill Guerin">Bill Guerin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Knuble" title="Mike Knuble">Mike Knuble</a> and the newly acquired <a href="/wiki/Glen_Murray_(hockey_player)" class="mw-redirect" title="Glen Murray (hockey player)">Glen Murray</a>. Their regular season success did not translate to the post-season, however, as they lost in six games to the underdog eighth-place Montreal Canadiens in the first round.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2002%E2%80%9303_NHL_season" title="2002–03 NHL season">2002–03</a> season found the Bruins platooning their goaltending staff between <a href="/wiki/Steve_Shields_(hockey_player)" class="mw-redirect" title="Steve Shields (hockey player)">Steve Shields</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Grahame" title="John Grahame">John Grahame</a> for most of the season. A mid-season trade brought in veteran <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Hackett" title="Jeff Hackett">Jeff Hackett</a>. In the midst of a late-season slump, General Manager Mike O'Connell fired head coach <a href="/wiki/Robbie_Ftorek" title="Robbie Ftorek">Robbie Ftorek</a> with nine games to go and named himself interim coach. The Bruins managed to finish seventh in the East, but lost to the eventual Stanley Cup champion <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Devils" title="New Jersey Devils">New Jersey Devils</a> in five games.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_NHL_season" title="2003–04 NHL season">2003–04</a>, the Bruins began the season with ex-<a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leaf</a> goalie <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Potvin" title="Félix Potvin">Felix Potvin</a>. Later in the season, the Bruins put <a href="/wiki/Rookie" title="Rookie">rookie</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Raycroft" title="Andrew Raycroft">Andrew Raycroft</a> into the starting role. Raycroft eventually won the Calder Trophy that season. The Bruins went on to win another division title and appeared to get past the first round for the first time in five years with a 3–1 series lead on the rival Canadiens. The Canadiens rallied back, however, to win three straight games, upsetting the Bruins.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_NHL_season" title="2004–05 NHL season">2004–05 NHL season</a> was wiped out by a <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_NHL_lockout" title="2004–05 NHL lockout">lockout</a>, and the Bruins had a lot of space within the new salary cap implemented for <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_NHL_season" title="2005–06 NHL season">2005–06</a>. Bruins management eschewed younger free agents in favor of older veterans such as <a href="/wiki/Alexei_Zhamnov" title="Alexei Zhamnov">Alexei Zhamnov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Leetch" title="Brian Leetch">Brian Leetch</a>. The newcomers were oft-injured, and by the end of November, the Bruins team traded their captain and franchise player, Joe Thornton (who went on to win the Art Ross and Hart Trophies). In exchange, the Bruins received <a href="/wiki/Marco_Sturm" title="Marco Sturm">Marco Sturm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brad_Stuart" title="Brad Stuart">Brad Stuart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Primeau" title="Wayne Primeau">Wayne Primeau</a> from the <a href="/wiki/San_Jose_Sharks" title="San Jose Sharks">San Jose Sharks</a>. After losing 10 of 11 games before the trade, the Bruins came back with a 3–0 victory over the league-leading <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Senators" title="Ottawa Senators">Ottawa Senators</a>, as rookie goaltender <a href="/wiki/Hannu_Toivonen" title="Hannu Toivonen">Hannu Toivonen</a> earned his first career NHL shutout. When Toivonen went down with an injury in January, journeyman goalie <a href="/wiki/Tim_Thomas_(ice_hockey)" title="Tim Thomas (ice hockey)">Tim Thomas</a> started 16-straight games and brought the Bruins back into the playoff run. Two points out of eighth place at the Winter Olympic break, the Bruins fired General Manager <a href="/wiki/Mike_O%27Connell" title="Mike O'Connell">Mike O'Connell</a> in March and the Bruins missed the playoffs for the first time in five years.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Chiarelli_(ice_hockey)" title="Peter Chiarelli (ice hockey)">Peter Chiarelli</a> was hired as the new GM of the team. Head coach <a href="/wiki/Mike_Sullivan_(ice_hockey_b._1968)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mike Sullivan (ice hockey b. 1968)">Mike Sullivan</a> was fired and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Lewis_(ice_hockey)" title="Dave Lewis (ice hockey)">Dave Lewis</a>, former coach of the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Red_Wings" title="Detroit Red Wings">Detroit Red Wings</a>, was hired to replace him. The Bruins signed <a href="/wiki/Zdeno_Ch%C3%A1ra" title="Zdeno Chára">Zdeno Chara</a>, one of the most coveted defensemen in the NHL and a former NHL All-Star, from the Ottawa Senators, and <a href="/wiki/Marc_Savard" title="Marc Savard">Marc Savard</a>, who finished just three points short of a 100-point season in <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_NHL_season" title="2005–06 NHL season">2005–06</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Thrashers" title="Atlanta Thrashers">Atlanta Thrashers</a>, to long-term deals.</p>
<p>The 2006–07 season ended in the team finishing in last place in the division. The Bruins traded Brad Stuart and Wayne Primeau to the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Flames" title="Calgary Flames">Calgary Flames</a> for <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ference" title="Andrew Ference">Andrew Ference</a> and forward <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Kobasew" title="Chuck Kobasew">Chuck Kobasew</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rejuvenation_in_Boston">Rejuvenation in Boston</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rejuvenation in Boston">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the disappointing <a href="/wiki/2006%E2%80%9307_NHL_season" title="2006–07 NHL season">2007 season</a>, Lewis was fired as coach, and the Bruins announced on June 21, 2007, that former Canadiens/Devils head coach <a href="/wiki/Claude_Julien_(ice_hockey)" title="Claude Julien (ice hockey)">Claude Julien</a> had been named as the new head coach.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The Bruins also unveiled a new logo and a brand new shoulder patch closely based on the main jersey logo used until 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_NHL_season" title="2007–08 NHL season">2008 campaign</a> saw the Bruins regain some respectability, finishing 41–29–12 and making the playoffs. The season ended on a bright note for the Bruins when they forced the Montreal Canadiens to play a seven-game playoff series, including a memorable Game 6 in which Boston came back to win 5–4. Their performance, despite a 5–0 loss in the seventh game, rekindled interest in the team in New England, where the Bruins had for years been heavily overshadowed by the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Red Sox</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_England_Patriots" title="New England Patriots">Patriots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boston_Celtics" title="Boston Celtics">Celtics</a>, all of whom had recently won championships in their respective leagues. Although Bruins center <a href="/wiki/Patrice_Bergeron" title="Patrice Bergeron">Patrice Bergeron</a> was injured with a concussion most of the season, youngsters <a href="/wiki/Milan_Lucic" title="Milan Lucic">Milan Lucic</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Krej%C4%8D%C3%AD" title="David Krejčí">David Krejci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladim%C3%ADr_Sobotka" title="Vladimír Sobotka">Vladimir Sobotka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petteri_Nokelainen" title="Petteri Nokelainen">Petteri Nokelainen</a> showed promise in the playoffs.</p>
<p>After a slow start to the <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9309_NHL_season" title="2008–09 NHL season">2008–09 season</a>, the Bruins won 17 of their next 20 games, leading many to see them as a revival of the "Big Bad Bruins" from the 1970s and 1980s. During the 2009 All-Star Weekend's Skills Competition, captain Zdeno Chara fired the NHL's then-fastest measured "hardest shot" ever, with a clocked in speed of 105.4&#160;mph (169.7&#160;km/h) velocity. (Chara has since broken his own record three times, two of those on the same night.) The number of injured players in the season saw many call-ups from the Bruins' <a href="/wiki/American_Hockey_League" title="American Hockey League">American Hockey League</a> (AHL) <a href="/wiki/Providence_Bruins" title="Providence Bruins">Providence Bruins</a> farm team, with rookie defenseman <a href="/wiki/Matt_Hunwick" title="Matt Hunwick">Matt Hunwick</a> and forward <a href="/wiki/Byron_Bitz" title="Byron Bitz">Byron Bitz</a> seeing success. The Bruins went on to have the best record in the Eastern Conference and qualified for the playoffs for the fifth time in nine years, facing the Canadiens in the playoffs for the fourth time during that span, defeating them in four games before losing in seven games to the <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Hurricanes" title="Carolina Hurricanes">Carolina Hurricanes</a> in the conference semi-finals.</p>
<p>The 2009 summer off-season saw the departure of long-time defensive forward <a href="/wiki/P._J._Axelsson" title="P. J. Axelsson">P. J. Axelsson</a> from Sweden, who signed a multi-year contract<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> with his hometown <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%B6lunda_HC" title="Frölunda HC">Frölunda HC</a> team. With Toronto Maple Leafs General Manager <a href="/wiki/Brian_Burke_(ice_hockey)" title="Brian Burke (ice hockey)">Brian Burke</a> threatening an offer sheet and Bruins management unable to meet his salary demands, forward <a href="/wiki/Phil_Kessel" title="Phil Kessel">Phil Kessel</a> was traded to Toronto for a trio of future draft picks.</p>
<p>On January 1, 2010, the Bruins won the <a href="/wiki/2010_NHL_Winter_Classic" title="2010 NHL Winter Classic">2010 NHL Winter Classic</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Flyers" title="Philadelphia Flyers">Philadelphia Flyers</a> in a 2–1 overtime decision at <a href="/wiki/Fenway_Park" title="Fenway Park">Fenway Park</a>, thus becoming the first home team to win an outdoor classic game. However, following the New Year's Day game, the Bruins, hobbled by injuries, would go through a five-week-long period of lackluster play, with only two wins and compiling ten regulation losses earning them only eight points in the Eastern Conference standings in that 15-game long period, before breaking the losing streak in an away game against the Canadiens on February 7, with <a href="/wiki/Tuukka_Rask" title="Tuukka Rask">Tuukka Rask</a> shutting out the Habs 3–0. The win over the Canadiens was the first of four successive victories leading into the break in play for the NHL's participation in the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a>, and established Tuukka Rask as the number one goaltender for the Bruins, as <a href="/wiki/Tim_Thomas_(ice_hockey)" title="Tim Thomas (ice hockey)">Tim Thomas</a> would only start eight of the 22 games remaining in the post-Olympic period of the season, with Rask winning eight of his post-Olympic starts, including two shutouts. Thomas was on the silver medal-winning U.S. team, with Patrice Bergeron on the gold medal-winning Canadian team.</p>
<p>The importance of former <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Sabres" title="Buffalo Sabres">Buffalo Sabres</a> forward <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Paille" title="Daniel Paille">Daniel Paille</a>'s acquisition by the Bruins, and his emergence as a penalty-killing forward, was emphasized on April 10, 2010, as Paille, <a href="/wiki/Steve_B%C3%A9gin" title="Steve Bégin">Steve Begin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blake_Wheeler" title="Blake Wheeler">Blake Wheeler</a> combined for the first-ever known trio of <a href="/wiki/Short_handed" class="mw-redirect" title="Short handed">short handed</a> goals within one penalty kill, in only 1:04 of game time, in a home game against the Carolina Hurricanes, helping the Bruins to sixth place in the NHL Eastern Conference, and a 2010 NHL playoff opening round appearance against the Buffalo Sabres, which they won 4–2. Boston became only the third team in NHL history to lose a playoff series after leading 3–0 when they lost in Game 7 to the Philadelphia Flyers after losing a 3–0 lead in the second round on May 14, 2010, also losing the services of <a href="/wiki/Marco_Sturm" title="Marco Sturm">Marco Sturm</a> in the first game and playmaking center David Krejci to injury in the third game of the series.</p>
<p>On April 13, 2010, the Boston Bruins received the second overall draft pick for the <a href="/wiki/2010_NHL_Draft" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 NHL Draft">2010 NHL Draft</a>, having received it via the trade that sent Phil Kessel to the Toronto Maple Leafs. With the pick, the Bruins selected <a href="/wiki/Tyler_Seguin" title="Tyler Seguin">Tyler Seguin</a> on June 25, 2010. In other off-season moves, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Campbell_(ice_hockey)" title="Gregory Campbell (ice hockey)">Greg Campbell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Horton" title="Nathan Horton">Nathan Horton</a> joined the team, and Vladimir Sobotka and <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Wideman" title="Dennis Wideman">Dennis Wideman</a> left the Bruins in the 2010 free agency. After the season ended on June 16, 2010, <a href="/wiki/Cam_Neely" title="Cam Neely">Cam Neely</a> was named the new team president of the Bruins.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011.E2.80.9313:_Stanley_Cup_playoff_contenders">2011–13: Stanley Cup playoff contenders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boston_Bruins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2011–13: Stanley Cup playoff contenders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg/180px-Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg" width="180" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg/270px-Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg/360px-Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="437" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Milan_Lucic_Stanley_Cup_celebration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Milan_Lucic" title="Milan Lucic">Milan Lucic</a> holds the Stanley Cup in his hometown of <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a> after the Bruins defeated the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks" title="Vancouver Canucks">Vancouver Canucks</a> in Game 7 of the <a href="/wiki/2011_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="2011 Stanley Cup Finals">Finals</a>, their first since 1972.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On February 15, 2011, the Bruins acquired center <a href="/wiki/Chris_Kelly_(ice_hockey)" title="Chris Kelly (ice hockey)">Chris Kelly</a> from the Ottawa Senators after Marc Savard's attempted comeback ended due to another concussion; this one delivered in <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Avalanche" title="Colorado Avalanche">Colorado</a> by former Bruin Matt Hunwick. Ottawa received the Bruins' second-round pick in 2011. Just two days later and on the brink of the trade deadline, the Bruins acquired defenseman <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1%C5%A1_Kaberle" title="Tomáš Kaberle">Tomas Kaberle</a> in a trade from the Toronto Maple Leafs in exchange for prospect <a href="/wiki/Joe_Colborne" title="Joe Colborne">Joe Colborne</a>, a first round selection in <a href="/wiki/2011_NHL_Entry_Draft" title="2011 NHL Entry Draft">2011</a>, and a potential second round pick in 2012 (which became official on May 27 when the Bruins clinched a berth in the Stanley Cup Finals). <a href="/wiki/Mark_Stuart_(ice_hockey)" title="Mark Stuart (ice hockey)">Mark Stuart</a> and Blake Wheeler were also traded to the Atlanta Thrashers for <a href="/wiki/Rich_Peverley" title="Rich Peverley">Rich Peverley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boris_Val%C3%A1bik" title="Boris Valábik">Boris Valabik</a>.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/2011_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2011 Stanley Cup playoffs">2011 Stanley Cup playoffs</a>, the Bruins became the first team in NHL history to win a seven-game series without scoring a power-play goal, as they eliminated the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Canadiens" title="Montreal Canadiens">Montreal Canadiens</a> in seven games, and also won their first playoff series after trailing 2–0. On May 6, the Bruins swept the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Flyers" title="Philadelphia Flyers">Philadelphia Flyers</a> in four games to advance to the Eastern Conference Finals for the first time since <a href="/wiki/1992_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="1992 Stanley Cup playoffs">1992</a>. Boston then defeated the <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Lightning" title="Tampa Bay Lightning">Tampa Bay Lightning</a> in seven games and advanced to the <a href="/wiki/2011_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="2011 Stanley Cup Finals">Stanley Cup Finals</a> for the first time since <a href="/wiki/1990_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1990 Stanley Cup Finals">1990</a> to face the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks" title="Vancouver Canucks">Vancouver Canucks</a>.</p>
<p>The Bruins lost the first two games of the series in very close contests, 1–0, on a goal with less than 19 seconds left in regulation, and then 3–2 in overtime. Game 3 did not start well for the Bruins either, as they lost Nathan Horton to injury at the 5:07 mark of the first period following a late hit by Canucks defenseman <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Rome" title="Aaron Rome">Aaron Rome</a> that left Horton prone on the ice for nearly 10 minutes. Despite losing Horton to a devastating hit, the Bruins defeated the Canucks, with four goals in each of the second and third periods, twice scoring short-handed goals, and going on to win, 8–1. It was the highest score by one team, and largest winning margin, in a Finals game since <a href="/wiki/1996_Stanley_Cup_Finals" title="1996 Stanley Cup Finals">1996</a>. Game 4 saw the Bruins defeating the Canucks in a 4–0 shutout. The home team continued to be the winner, with Game 5 in Vancouver going to the Canucks in a 1–0 shutout, then Game 6 going to the Bruins, who staved off elimination with a 5–2 defeat of the Canucks. The Bruins set a new record for the quickest four goals ever in a playoff series game, scoring in only 4:14 of game time in the first period of Game 6. Game 7, which was played in Vancouver on June 15, was the first time the Bruins have ever played in Game 7 of the Stanley Cup Finals. The Bruins shut out Vancouver 4–0 after two goals each from Bergeron and Marchand, winning the sixth Stanley Cup in franchise history and breaking a 39-year Cup drought. The 2010–11 Bruins were the first team in NHL history to win a Game 7 three times in the same playoff run. The championship also meant that all four Boston teams had won their respective championships at least once in the previous decade after the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Red Sox</a> won two World Series, the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Patriots" title="New England Patriots">Patriots</a> won three Super Bowls and the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Celtics" title="Boston Celtics">Celtics</a> recaptured an NBA championship. Following the Stanley Cup Finals, <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Globe" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Globe">Boston Globe</a></i> columnist <a href="/wiki/Dan_Shaughnessy" title="Dan Shaughnessy">Dan Shaughnessy</a> ranked all seven championships during the decade and ranked the Bruins' 2011 Stanley Cup triumph as third, behind only the Patriots winning <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXXVI" title="Super Bowl XXXVI">Super Bowl XXXVI</a> (second) and the Red Sox winning the <a href="/wiki/2004_World_Series" title="2004 World Series">2004 World Series</a> (first, breaking the "<a href="/wiki/Curse_of_the_Bambino" title="Curse of the Bambino">Curse of the Bambino</a>".)<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following their Stanley Cup win, the Bruins lost <a href="/wiki/Mark_Recchi" title="Mark Recchi">Mark Recchi</a> to retirement, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ryder" title="Michael Ryder">Michael Ryder</a> and Tomas Kaberle to free agency. The Bruins tweaked their roster by adding defenseman <a href="/wiki/Joe_Corvo" title="Joe Corvo">Joe Corvo</a> and forward <a href="/wiki/Beno%C3%AEt_Pouliot" title="Benoît Pouliot">Benoit Pouliot</a>. The Bruins' short summer took its toll early, going 3–7–0 in the month of October, before responding with a 21–3–1 record to cap off 2011, highlighted by a near-perfect November in which the team did not lose in regulation. Sophomore forward <a href="/wiki/Tyler_Seguin" title="Tyler Seguin">Tyler Seguin</a> shone the brightest during the streak, eventually paving the way for his first <a href="/wiki/59th_National_Hockey_League_All-Star_Game" title="59th National Hockey League All-Star Game">All-Star Game</a> selection, joining teammates <a href="/wiki/Zdeno_Ch%C3%A1ra" title="Zdeno Chára">Zdeno Chara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tim_Thomas_(ice_hockey)" title="Tim Thomas (ice hockey)">Tim Thomas</a> on Team Chara. On January 23, Thomas caused a stir by not attending the Bruins' visit to the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, saying the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">government</a> had "grown out of control." After an 8–4–1 record to begin 2012, the Bruins' inconsistent form resurfaced for much of February and March, during which they endured key injuries to Nathan Horton (concussion) and <a href="/wiki/Tuukka_Rask" title="Tuukka Rask">Tuukka Rask</a> (groin), and an impotent power play. At the February 27 trading deadline they traded for <a href="/wiki/Greg_Zanon" title="Greg Zanon">Greg Zanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Mottau" title="Mike Mottau">Mike Mottau</a> and one-time Bruin <a href="/wiki/Brian_Rolston" title="Brian Rolston">Brian Rolston</a>, then signed goalie <a href="/wiki/Marty_Turco" title="Marty Turco">Marty Turco</a> to add goaltending depth. The Bruins went on to finish second in the Eastern Conference with 102 points, winning the Northeast Division title.</p>
<p>They faced the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Capitals" title="Washington Capitals">Washington Capitals</a> in the first round of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2012 Stanley Cup playoffs">2012 Stanley Cup playoffs</a>. In a seven-game series which all of the games were decided by only one goal, the Bruins came up short against rookie goalie <a href="/wiki/Braden_Holtby" title="Braden Holtby">Braden Holtby</a> and head coach <a href="/wiki/Dale_Hunter" title="Dale Hunter">Dale Hunter</a>'s defensive-minded game plan. The seventh game saw <a href="/wiki/Joel_Ward_(ice_hockey)" title="Joel Ward (ice hockey)">Joel Ward</a> deflect the series-winning goal past Tim Thomas in overtime to give the Capitals the victory and end the Bruins' season.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG/220px-Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG/330px-Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG/440px-Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1754" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boston_Bruins_Prince_of_Wales_Trophy_2013-06-07.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
2013 Eastern Conference champions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the off-season preceding the <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_NHL_lockout" title="2012–13 NHL lockout">lockout</a>, Tim Thomas made his decision to sit out the <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_NHL_season" title="2012–13 NHL season">2012–13 season</a>. General Manager <a href="/wiki/Peter_Chiarelli_(ice_hockey)" title="Peter Chiarelli (ice hockey)">Peter Chiarelli</a> confirmed Thomas' decision. Thomas was first suspended for not reporting to training camp, then his rights were traded to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Islanders" title="New York Islanders">New York Islanders</a> on February 7. The Bruins decided to go with the goaltending pair of Tuukka Rask and <a href="/wiki/Anton_Khudobin" title="Anton Khudobin">Anton Khudobin</a> for the season. Meanwhile, highly touted prospect <a href="/wiki/Dougie_Hamilton" title="Dougie Hamilton">Dougie Hamilton</a> was promoted to the main roster after spending a season in the juniors.</p>
<p>The Bruins battled the Montreal Canadiens for leadership in the Northeast Division all season, before a loss to the Ottawa Senators in a make-up game following the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Marathon_bombing" title="Boston Marathon bombing">Boston Marathon bombing</a> on April 28 gave the Canadiens the division title. Boston settled for fourth place in the Eastern Conference standings with 62 points. On April 2, the Bruins acquired former All-Star <a href="/wiki/Jarom%C3%ADr_J%C3%A1gr" title="Jaromír Jágr">Jaromir Jagr</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Stars" title="Dallas Stars">Dallas Stars</a>, after failing to acquire <a href="/wiki/Jarome_Iginla" title="Jarome Iginla">Jarome Iginla</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Calgary_Flames" title="Calgary Flames">Calgary Flames</a>. Jagr would end up being a winger for the <a href="/wiki/Patrice_Bergeron" title="Patrice Bergeron">Patrice Bergeron</a>–<a href="/wiki/Brad_Marchand" title="Brad Marchand">Brad Marchand</a> forward line, as usual winger Tyler Seguin was transferred to the third line with Chris Kelly and Rich Peverley, to give a more even scoring threat across all four forward lines for the Bruins. In the opening round of the <a href="/wiki/2013_Stanley_Cup_playoffs" title="2013 Stanley Cup playoffs">2013 playoffs</a>, the Bruins took on the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Maple_Leafs" title="Toronto Maple Leafs">Toronto Maple Leafs</a>. After leading the series 3–1 with a pair of wins in Toronto, the Maple Leafs won Games 5 and 6 to force Game 7 in Boston. Toronto led 4–1 in the third period of the decider, before the Bruins came back late with three goals. A goal from Nathan Horton 9:18 into the period cut the deficit to two, but the Bruins were unable to cut more into the lead until late in the period, when Milan Lucic and Patrice Bergeron scored at 18:38 and 19:09 respectively with the goalie pulled to tie the game at four. Bergeron would score again in overtime, netting the series winner to eliminate the Maple Leafs. Boston's Game 7 win marked the first time a team came back from a three-goal deficit in the third period to win a playoff game. In the second round, Boston led 3–0 against the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a> before winning the series in five games. Boston then defeated the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins" title="Pittsburgh Penguins">Pittsburgh Penguins</a> in the Eastern Conference Finals in a four-game sweep to advance to the Stanley Cup Finals.</p>
